Maintenance of a Waqf House: Obligation of the Dweller
Ruling with proof and detailed application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who must maintain a waqf house and apply the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' to determine the obligation.

Core Idea
The dweller bears maintenance because profit goes with liability.

A common question in waqf law is: who pays for repairs? If a house is endowed and someone has the right to live in it, is the waqf itself responsible or the resident? This lesson examines a clear Hanafi ruling: the resident must maintain the property. We'll explore the rational proof behind this and see how the principle 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' governs the allocation of costs and benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The maintenance ('imarah) of a waqf house. Who is obligated? The person who has the right of dwelling (sukna). The ruling is that maintenance is wajib (obligatory) upon that person.
The ruling is not that the waqf itself must maintain.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author provides a rational proof: 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profit is with liability). This is a legal maxim. He also gives an analogy: it is like the expense of a slave bequeathed for his service—the one who benefits must bear costs.
The proof is aqli (rational), not naqli (textual).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof proves the ruling)
The maxim means whoever takes the benefit (kharaj) also bears the risk of loss (daman). The dweller enjoys the use of the house, so he must bear the maintenance costs. The analogy to the slave: if someone is given a slave's service, he must feed the slave; similarly, the dweller must maintain the house.
The risk here is that the property may deteriorate; the dweller bears that risk by maintaining.
4
Examine the details from al-Zahiriyyah
The text elaborates on what happens if the dweller makes improvements and then dies. The key rule: if removal of improvements would damage the building, the next dweller must compensate the heirs (or the house is rented until compensation is paid). For minor repairs (plastering, mudding), there is no compensation at all.
This protects both the waqf (preserving the building) and the heirs' investment.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi ruling is that maintenance is on the beneficiary. Other schools may place it on the waqf itself. The practical difference: in a dispute, the Hanafi position requires the dweller to pay, whereas others would take from waqf revenues.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both approaches aim to preserve the waqf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو داراً فعمارته على من له السكني) أي لو كان الموقوف داراً فعمارة الموقوف على من له سكناه لأن الخراج بالضمان وصار كنفقة العبد الموصي بخدمته. وفي الظهيرية فإن كان المشروط له السكنى رم حيطان الدار الموقوفة بالآجر وجصصها أو أدخل فيها أجذاعاً ثم مات ولا يمكن نزع شيء من ذلك إلا بتضرر بالبناء فليس للورثة أخذ شيء من ذلك ولكن يقال للمشروط له السكني بعده إضمن لورثته قيمة البناء ولك السكنى، فإن أبى أوجرت الدار وصرفت الغلة إلى ورثة الميت بقدر قيمة البناء، فإذا وفت غلته بقيمة البناء أعيد السكنى إلى من له السكنى وليس لصاحب السكني أن يرضى بقلع ذلك وهدمه وإن كان ما رم الأول مثل تجصيص الحيطان أو تطيين السطوح أو ما أشبه ذلك ثم مات الأول فليس للورثة أن يرجعوا بشيء من ذلك، ألا تری أن رجلاً لو اشتری داراً وجصصها وطين سطوحها ثم استحقت الدار لا يكون للمشتري أن البائع بقيمة الجص ،والطين وإنما يكون له أن يرجع بقيمة ما يمكنه أن ينقضه ويسلم نقضه إليه اهـ.
His saying: (And if a house, then its maintenance is upon the one who has the right of dwelling) meaning if the endowed [property] is a house, then the maintenance of the endowed [property] is upon the one who has the right of dwelling in it, because the tax is due with liability, and it is like the expense of a slave bequeathed for his service. And in al-Zahiriyyah: If the one for whom dwelling is conditioned repaired the walls of the endowed house with bricks and plastered them, or inserted beams into it, and then died, and it is not possible to remove any of that except with damage to the building, then the heirs have no right to take anything of that. But it is said to the one for whom dwelling is conditioned after him: 'Pay the value of the building to his heirs, and you have the dwelling.' If he refuses, the house is rented out, and its yield is spent on the heirs of the deceased to the extent of the value of the building. When its yield fulfills the value of the building, the dwelling is returned to the one who has the right of dwelling. And it is not permissible for the owner of the dwelling to agree to the removal and demolition of that, even if what the first repaired was like plastering the walls or mudding the roofs or something similar, and then the first died; the heirs have no recourse for anything of that. Do you not see that if a man bought a house and plastered it and mudded its roofs, and then the house was reclaimed, the buyer cannot claim from the seller the value of the plaster and mud, but he can only claim the value of what he can remove and hand over its removal to him? End quote.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage explains the rule that the person who has the right to dwell in a waqf house (e.g., a beneficiary) is responsible for its maintenance. The principle is 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profit is with liability) — the one who enjoys the benefit bears the cost of upkeep. The text from al-Zahiriyyah elaborates: if the resident makes improvements (bricks, plaster, beams) and then dies, the heirs cannot take them because the building is damaged by removal. Instead, the next resident must pay the value of the improvements to the heirs or else the house is rented out until the heirs are compensated, after which the right to dwell returns. Even for minor repairs like plastering, the heirs get nothing. The analogy of a buyer who improves a house later reclaimed by its true owner illustrates that only removable improvements can be reclaimed. This ruling ensures that waqf property is preserved while protecting the resident's investment.

Key Terms
السكنى (al-suknā): The right of dwelling in a property, often granted as a waqf benefit
الخراج بالضمان (al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān): A legal maxim: the one who bears the risk of loss (daman) gets the profit (kharaj); here, the dweller bears risk so must maintain
الظهيرية (al-Zahiriyyah): A reference to a text or compilation in the Hanafi school (possibly al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Zahiriyyah, what happens if the dweller makes substantial improvements (e.g., brick walls, plaster, beams) and then dies?
A) His heirs may remove the improvements even if it damages the building
B) The next resident must pay the heirs the value of the improvements or the house is rented until compensated
C) The improvements become the property of the waqf and heirs get nothing
D) The heirs can force the next resident to demolish the improvements

Key Takeaway
The dweller in a waqf house must maintain it because profit goes with liability; substantial improvements by a deceased dweller must be compensated by the next resident, preserving the waqf and preventing injustice.

Tenant Building in Waqf Shop Without Permission
Ruling with proof on tenant building in waqf shop

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a tenant who builds on waqf property without permission, and apply the conditions for removal or compensation.

Core Idea
A tenant who builds without permission must remove it if harmless; if harmful, the trustee takes it at the lower value.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from al-Mujtaba regarding a tenant (musta'jir) who adds a structure to a waqf shop without the trustee's permission. The ruling balances the inviolability of waqf property with the tenant's investment, using qiyas with the case of repairing another's house without consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principal scenario
Recognize the core situation: a tenant builds on waqf property without the trustee's permission.
2
Understand the two sub-scenarios
Distinguish based on harm: if removal does not harm the waqf, the tenant must remove at his own cost; if removal harms, the trustee takes ownership at the lower of two values (removed or not removed).
The lower value protects the waqf from overcompensation.
3
Apply the qiyas
The ruling is analogous to repairing another's house without permission: the same principle applies.
Qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Note the additional scenario
Building a room on top of the shop is permissible only if it does not damage the original and either increases rent or is necessary for renting.
Otherwise it is not allowed.
5
Extract the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position from al-Mujtaba: tenant must remove if no harm; if harm, trustee takes at lower value.
This is from the later fatawa literature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجعل في المجتبى مسألة ما إذا عمرها ومات نظير ما إذا عمر دار غيره بغير إذنه ثم قال مستأجر حانوت الوقف بني فيه بغير إذن القيم لا يرجع عليه ويرفع بناءه إن لم يضر بالوقف وإلا يتملكه القيم بأقل القيمتين منزوعاً وغير منزوع، فإن أبي يتربص إلى أن يخلص ماله. ثم قال : مستأجر الوقف بني غرفة على الحانوت إن لم يضر بأصله ويزيد في أجرته أو لا يستأجر إلا بالغرفة يجوز وإلا فلا .اهـ.
And he made in al-Mujtaba the case of when he repairs it and dies analogous to the case of when one repairs another's house without his permission. Then he said: A tenant of a waqf shop who builds in it without the permission of the caretaker has no recourse against him, and he removes his building if it does not harm the waqf; otherwise, the caretaker takes ownership of it at the lesser of the two values, removed and not removed. If he refuses, he waits until his money is recovered. Then he said: For the tenant of the waqf who builds a room on top of the shop, if it does not harm the original structure and increases its rent, or if the shop cannot be rented except with the room, it is permissible; otherwise, it is not. End quote.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from al-Mujtaba addresses a tenant (musta'jir) who builds on waqf property without the trustee's permission. Two scenarios: (1) If the building (e.g., a structure inside the shop) can be removed without damaging the waqf property, the tenant must remove it at his own expense and has no right to reimbursement. (2) If removal would harm the waqf, the trustee may take ownership of the building, paying the tenant the lower of two values: the value if removed (lower) or the value if left in place. If the tenant refuses the payment, he must wait until he recovers his investment from the rent (possibly by staying). Additionally, if the tenant builds an extra room that enhances the property and increases rent, and especially if the shop cannot be rented otherwise, it is permissible with the trustee's approval. Otherwise, it is not allowed. This protects the waqf from unauthorized alterations while balancing the tenant's investment.

Key Terms
مستأجر (mustaʾjir): Tenant, lessee of the waqf property
القيم (al-qayyim): The trustee or caretaker of the waqf
المجتبى (al-Mujtaba): A Hanafi fiqh work by Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri (or later compilation), used as a source for fatwa

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a tenant builds a structure in a waqf shop without the trustee's permission, and removal would harm the waqf, what happens?
A) The tenant must remove it anyway at his own cost
B) The trustee takes ownership of the structure at the higher of the two values (removed or not removed)
C) The trustee takes ownership at the lower of the two values
D) The tenant keeps the structure and pays extra rent

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school protects waqf property by requiring unauthorized building to be removed if possible, and if not, compensating the tenant at the lower value to prevent unjust enrichment.

Improvements to Waqf Property: Inseparable vs Removable
Ruling on improvements by a waqf beneficiary's descendant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inseparable improvements (that become part of the waqf) and removable improvements (that may be reclaimed) and apply the condition of no harm to the waqf.

Core Idea
Inseparable improvements belong to the waqf; removable items may be reclaimed if no harm.

This passage from al-Qunyah addresses a common waqf dispute: one beneficiary makes improvements to the endowed property, and another wants to occupy his share without paying. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling based on the nature of the improvement—whether it is inseparable (like plastering) or removable (like bricks). The ruling preserves the integrity of the waqf while allowing the improver to reclaim what can be taken without damage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the waqf structure and the act
The waqf is a house endowed on a man and his descendants, then to the poor. One descendant (a grandchild) built, mudded, plastered, and laid bricks in part of it. Another descendant wants to dwell in his share but is refused until payment for the improvements.
Note that the improver is not the direct beneficiary (the man's child) but a grandchild.
2
Classify the improvements
The text lists three types: mudding (tayyin), plastering (jass), and laying bricks (bast al-ājurr). The first two are inseparable; bricks are removable. The ruling depends on this classification.
Inseparability means the improvement cannot be removed without destroying or damaging the original structure.
3
Apply the rule: inseparable become waqf
Mudding and plastering become part of the waqf (taba‘an lil-waqf). The improver cannot demand compensation for them because they are now property of the endowment.
This prevents unjust enrichment and preserves the waqf's purpose.
4
Determine the exception for removable items
Bricks (ājurr) can be removed by the builder if doing so does not harm the waqf building. The condition 'no harm' is critical—if removal would damage the structure, it is not permitted.
The right to remove is not absolute; it is conditional on the absence of damage.
5
Apply the qiyas (analogy) to the public shop
The text draws an analogy to a person who builds in a musabbal (public shop). If the builder's addition can be removed without harming the original building, he may take it; otherwise not. This confirms the same rule for waqf.
Qiyas jaliy: the common illa is the ability to remove without damage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : لو وقف داراً على رجل وأولاده وأولاد أولاده أبداً ما تناسلوا فإذا انقطعت فإلى الفقراء، ثم بنى واحد من أولاد أولاد الموقوف عليهم بعض الدار الموقوفة وطين البعض وجصص البعض وبسط فيه الآجر فطلب الآخر منه حصته ليسكن فيها فمنعه منها حتى يدفع له حصة ما أنفق فيها ليس له ذلك، والتطيين والجص صار تبعاً للوقف، وله أن ينقض الآجر . قال رضي الله عنه : وإنما ينقض الآجر إذا لم يكن في نقضه ضرر بالوقف كمن بنى في الحانوت المسبل فله رفعه إذا لم يضر بالبناء القديم وإلا فلا اهـ.
And in al-Qunyah: If a house is endowed upon a man and his children and his grandchildren forever as long as they procreate, and when [the line] ends then to the poor; then one of the grandchildren of the beneficiaries built part of the endowed house, and mudded some, plastered some, and laid bricks in it; then another asked him for his share to dwell in it, but he prevented him from it until he pays him his share of what he spent; he does not have that right. The mudding and plastering become part of the waqf, and he may remove the bricks. He, may Allah be pleased with him, said: He only removes the bricks if there is no harm in their removal to the waqf, like one who builds in a public shop; he may remove it if it does not harm the old building; otherwise no.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from al-Qunyah deals with a situation where a descendant beneficiary of a waqf house (e.g., a grandchild) makes improvements (mudding, plastering, laying bricks) to part of the house. Another beneficiary asks to dwell in his share but is prevented until he pays for the improvements. The ruling: the descendant has no right to demand payment because the plastering and mudding become part of the waqf (they are inseparable). However, the bricks (a removable item) may be taken back by the builder, provided that removing them does not harm the waqf building. The analogy is drawn to a person who builds in a public shop (mushaffa'): if the removal does not damage the original structure, it is allowed; otherwise, it is not. This preserves the waqf while allowing the remover to reclaim removable property.

Key Terms
المسبل (al-musabbal): A public property or endowment that is made available for public use (like a shop).
الآجر (al-ājurr): Bricks, specifically baked bricks used in construction.
التطيين (al-taṭyīn): Mudding, a process of applying mud or clay to walls as plaster.
الجص (al-jiṣṣ): Plastering with gypsum or lime plaster.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the mudding and plastering done by a descendant-beneficiary in a waqf house?
A) The descendant must be paid for them by the other beneficiaries.
B) They become part of the waqf and cannot be removed or compensated.
C) They can be removed by the descendant if he pays for repairs.
D) They remain the property of the descendant and can be taken back anytime.

Key Takeaway
In waqf, inseparable improvements become part of the endowment; removable items may be reclaimed only if no harm to the waqf structure.

Caretaker's Wage and Mosque Maintenance
Two rulings from al-Qunyah about waqf caretaker and mosque maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a waqf caretaker can claim additional wages for maintenance work and explain the reasoning (qawamah).

Core Idea
A caretaker cannot double-dip: his guardianship wage covers all maintenance duties.

This lesson examines two rulings from al-Qunyah concerning the caretaker (qayyim) of a waqf property. The first ruling clarifies that the caretaker's compensation for his role already encompasses any maintenance work he performs, so he is not entitled to an extra wage. The second ruling addresses the use of mosque funds to repair a neighboring market roof that affects the mosque. These rulings illustrate the fiduciary duties of a waqf caretaker and the principles of expense allocation in waqf administration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal issues
The passage addresses two distinct cases: (a) caretaker's entitlement to wages for maintenance, and (b) use of mosque funds to repair a neighboring market roof.
2
Analyze the first ruling
Focus on the phrase 'لا يجتمع له أجر القوامة وأجر العمل'. Understand that the caretaker's role (qawamah) includes a comprehensive wage that covers all necessary work; therefore, he cannot claim an additional wage for specific maintenance tasks.
The ruling is from al-Qunyah, a later Hanafi fatwa collection.
3
Analyze the second ruling
Note the condition: the market roof is uncovered, causing heat to enter the summer mosque. The caretaker may use mosque wealth to cover the market roof to the extent needed to repel the harm.
This illustrates that waqf funds can be used for adjacent property if it protects the waqf.
4
Synthesize the underlying principles
Both rulings highlight the caretaker's fiduciary duty: he cannot profit beyond his fixed wage, and he must use waqf funds prudently to protect the waqf's purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسعة عشرة : إذا عمل القيم في عمارة المسجد والوقف كعمل الأجير لا يستحق أجراً لأنه لا يجتمع له أجر القوامة وأجر العمل؛ كذا في القنية وسيأتي أيضاً العشرون لو انكشف سقف السوق فغلب الحر على المسجد الصيفي لوقوع الشمس فيه فللقيم ستر سقف السوق من مال المسجد بقدر ما يندفع به هذا القدر ؛ كذا في القنية
The nineteenth [issue]: If the caretaker works in the maintenance of the mosque and the waqf like the work of a hired laborer, he does not deserve a wage because he cannot combine the wage of guardianship and the wage of work. Likewise in al-Qunyah, and also will come the twentieth: If the ceiling of the market is uncovered and the heat dominates the summer mosque due to the sun falling in it, then the caretaker may cover the ceiling of the market from the mosque's wealth to the extent that this amount is repelled. Likewise in al-Qunyah.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage contains two separate rulings from al-Qunyah, a later Hanafi fatwa collection. The nineteenth ruling states that the caretaker (qayyim) of a mosque or waqf, if he performs maintenance work, is not entitled to an additional wage for that work. The reason is that his position as caretaker already includes compensation (ujrat al-qawamah), and he cannot collect two wages for the same role. The work is considered part of his duties. The twentieth ruling deals with a situation where the sun enters a summer mosque because the market roof next to it is damaged. The caretaker is permitted to use mosque funds to cover the market roof to the extent necessary to protect the mosque from heat. This is a practical ruling on the use of waqf funds for neighboring property that directly impacts the mosque.

Key Terms
القيم (al-qayyim): The caretaker or manager of a waqf property or mosque
القوامة (al-qawamah): The office or function of being a caretaker, including the compensation for that role
الأجير (al-ajir): A hired laborer who works for wages

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because maintenance work is not considered a valid service
B) Because he already receives compensation for his role as caretaker and cannot combine two wages
C) Because the waqf funds are insufficient
D) Because the work is voluntary

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf law, the caretaker's compensation covers all maintenance duties, and he may also use waqf funds for adjacent structures when necessary to protect the waqf property.

Distinction Between Usufruct and Residence in Waqf
Principle that one with usufruct does not have residence right and vice versa, with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that rights of usufruct and residence in waqf are mutually exclusive, and apply it to determine the validity of renting to a beneficiary.

Core Idea
The one with usufruct cannot reside; the one with residence cannot lease — each right excludes the other.

In Hanafi waqf law, the rights of istighlal (usufruct, i.e., right to collect rent) and sukna (residence) are distinct and mutually exclusive. This distinction is not merely conceptual; it has practical consequences for the validity of contracts and the allocation of maintenance. In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the principle and proves it through the permissibility of renting the waqf property to the beneficiary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage states that the one with usufruct does not own the right of residence, and the one with residence does not own usufruct. This is the foundational rule.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The author cites the statement of early scholars: 'Renting the property to the beneficiary is valid.'
This is a qawl (saying) of the early scholars, not a direct hadith.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
If the beneficiary had the right of residence, renting to them would be invalid (since one cannot rent what one already owns). The validity of the rental proves they lack residence rights.
This is a logical inference from the permissibility of the contract.
4
Examine the practical question
What if the one with usufruct resides anyway? The author discusses whether rent is obligatory and to what purpose.
The answer depends on whether the waqf needs maintenance.
5
Reconcile the intra-Hanafi difference
The passage from al-Zahiriyyah states maintenance is on the one who deserves revenue; the author reconciles by interpreting that maintenance is taken from the revenue, not from personal funds.
This shows that apparent contradictions within the school are often resolved through careful interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظاهر كلام المصنف وغيره أن من له الاستغلال لا تكون العمارة عليه بناء على أن له الاستغلال لا يملك السكني. ومن له السكنى لا يملك الاستغلال كما صرح به في البزازية، وفي فتح القدير بقوله : وليس للموقوف عليهم الدار سكناها بل الاستغلال كما ليس للموقوف عليهم السكنى الاستغلال .اهـ. ويدل عليه قولهم إجارة العين للموقوف عليه صحيحة ومعلوم أن استئجار دار ممن له حق السكنى لا يجوز فجوازها دل على ما ذكرنا؛ كذا في البزازية. ولم أر حكم ما إذا سكن من له الاستغلال وفعل ما لا يجوز هل تجب الأجرة عليه ويأخذها المتولي ثم بدفعها إليه؟ والذي يظهر أن الوقف إن كان محتاجاً إلى العمارة وجبت الأجرة عليه فيأخذها المتولي ليعمر بها وإلا فلا فائدة في وجوبها حيث لم يكن له شريك في الغلة. وإنما لم تكن عليه لأن المتولي عليها يؤجرها ويعمرها بأجرتها كما لو أبي له السكنى، لكن في الظهيرية : وإذا صح الوقف واحتاج إلى العمارة فالعمارة على من يستحق الغلة اهـ. ويحمل على أن المعنى فالعمارة في غلتها ولما كانت غلتها له صار كان العمارة عليه.
And the apparent meaning of the author's statement and others is that the one who has usufruct (istighlal) is not responsible for maintenance, based on the fact that the one with usufruct does not own the right of residence. And the one with residence does not own usufruct, as declared in al-Bazzaziyyah. And in Fath al-Qadir with his words: 'The beneficiaries of the waqf house do not have its residence; rather they have its usufruct, just as those who have the residence do not have the usufruct.' End quote. And it is indicated by their statement: 'Renting the property to the beneficiary is valid'; and it is known that renting a house from one who has the right of residence is not valid, so its validity indicates what we have mentioned. Likewise in al-Bazzaziyyah. And I have not seen a ruling for if the one with usufruct resides and does what is not permissible: is rent obligatory upon him and the trustee takes it and then gives it to him? The apparent is that if the waqf needs maintenance, then rent is obligatory upon him and the trustee takes it to use for maintenance; otherwise there is no benefit in its obligation since he has no partner in the revenue. And it is not upon him because the trustee is responsible to rent it out and maintain it with its rent, as if he refused residence. However, in al-Zahiriyyah: 'When the waqf is valid and needs maintenance, the maintenance is upon the one who deserves the revenue (ghalla).' End quote. And it is interpreted to mean that the maintenance is [taken] from its revenue, and since the revenue is his, it becomes as if the maintenance is upon him.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage establishes a key distinction in Hanafi waqf law: the rights of istighlal (usufruct, i.e., right to collect rent) and sukna (residence) are mutually exclusive. A beneficiary entitled to usufruct cannot reside in the property, and a beneficiary entitled to residence cannot lease it out for profit. The proof from al-Bazzaziyyah is that it is valid to rent a waqf property to the beneficiary of usufruct; if he had the right of residence, renting to him would be invalid (since one cannot rent what one already owns). Thus, the validity of the rental confirms he does not have residence rights. The passage then addresses a practical question: what if the one with usufruct resides anyway? The author opines that if the waqf needs maintenance, he must pay rent (which the trustee collects for maintenance); if no maintenance is needed, there is no benefit in obliging rent. However, al-Zahiriyyah states that maintenance is on the one who deserves the revenue (ghalla), which seems to contradict. The reconciliation is that maintenance is actually taken from the revenue (ghalla) itself, and since the revenue belongs to the beneficiary, it is as if he bears the cost indirectly. This is an intra-Hanafi nuance.

Key Terms
الاستغلال (al-istighlal): The right to derive benefit from a waqf property, typically by leasing it and collecting rent
السكنى (al-sukna): The right to reside in a waqf property
الغلة (al-ghalla): The revenue or income generated from the waqf property, e.g., rent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do scholars prove that the beneficiary of usufruct (istighlal) does not have the right of residence (sukna)?
A) By analogy to a tenant
B) By the permissibility of renting the property to him, which would be invalid if he had residence rights
C) By a direct hadith from the Prophet
D) By the consensus of all Muslims

Key Takeaway
In waqf, the rights of usufruct and residence are mutually exclusive, proven by the validity of renting to the beneficiary.

intermediate
8 min
Mutual Exclusivity of Income and Dwelling in Waqf Beneficiaries
Commentary expanding the ruling on mutual exclusivity of usufruct and dwelling in waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the income beneficiary of a waqf cannot dwell in the property and apply the proof from the validity of renting to the beneficiary.

Core Idea
The right to income and the right to dwell in a waqf are mutually exclusive in the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines a key rule in Hanafi waqf law: the beneficiary entitled to the income (ghalla) of an endowed property does not have the right to dwell in it, and the beneficiary entitled to dwelling does not have the right to take the income. The commentator in al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq expands on this ruling, providing evidence from the validity of renting the property to the beneficiary and addressing intra-madhhab differences. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly managing waqf properties and avoiding invalid transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Ruling
The lesson opens with a clear statement: the income beneficiary cannot dwell, and the dweller cannot take income. Recognize this as the muftā bihi position.
2
Examine the Proof (Dalīl)
The proof is the validity of renting the waqf property to the income beneficiary. Why does this prove he does not own the dwelling? Because if he owned the dwelling, renting it to him would be impossible (you cannot lease what someone already owns).
This dalīl is cited from Abū Jaʿfar in al-Bazzāziyya.
3
Consider the Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Al-Khaṣṣāf initially equated the rights but later distinguished. Al-Shirnblāli holds the income beneficiary can dwell. Note that the preponderant view (al-rājiḥ) is mutual exclusivity.
The majority view is supported by al-Nawāzil, Fatḥ al-Qadīr, and al-Bazzāziyya.
4
Apply the Ruling to Subsidiary Issues
What if the income beneficiary unlawfully dwells? The passage discusses that if the waqf needs maintenance, the administrator can charge him rent and use it for repairs. Also, who can lease the property? Only the administrator or judge, not the beneficiary.
This is derived from the principle that the beneficiary does not own the ʿayn (asset).
5
Understand the Maintenance Implications
If the property needs repair, the administrator can rent it out to cover costs. The beneficiary cannot be forced to maintain because that would cause destruction of his property (itlāf mālihi).
This is analogous to the case of a sharecropper who refuses to sow seeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له الاستغلال لا يملك السكني. ومن له السكنى لا يملك الاستغلال كما صرح به في البزازية، وفي فتح القدير بقوله : وليس للموقوف عليهم الدار سكناها بل الاستغلال كما ليس للموقوف عليهم السكنى الاستغلال .اهـ. ويدل عليه قولهم إجارة العين للموقوف عليه صحيحة ومعلوم أن استئجار دار ممن له حق السكنى لا يجوز فجوازها دل على ما ذكرنا؛ كذا في البزازية. ولم أر حكم ما إذا سكن من له الاستغلال وفعل ما لا يجوز هل تجب الأجرة عليه ويأخذها المتولي ثم بدفعها إليه؟ والذي يظهر أن الوقف إن كان محتاجاً إلى العمارة وجبت الأجرة عليه فيأخذها المتولي ليعمر بها وإلا فلا فائدة في وجوبها حيث لم يكن له شريك في الغلة. وإنما لم تكن عليه لأن المتولي عليها يؤجرها ويعمرها بأجرتها كما لو أبي له السكنى، لكن في الظهيرية : وإذا صح الوقف واحتاج إلى العمارة فالعمارة على من يستحق الغلة اهـ. ويحمل على أن المعنى فالعمارة في غلتها ولما كانت غلتها له صار كان العمارة عليه. قال في الظهيرية : وإن كان المشروط له غلة الأرض جماعة رضي بعضهم بأن يرمه المتولي من مال الوقف وأبى البعض فمن أراد العمارة عمر المتولي حصته بحصته، ومن أبي تؤجر حصته وتصرف غلتها إلى العمارة إلى أن تحصل العمارة ثم تعاد إليه اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : ولو كان الواقف حين شرط الغلة لفلان ما عاش شرط على فلان مرمتها من يملك السكنى الخ) قد سوى بين المسألتين والثانية منهما وفاقية والأولى خلافية، والراجح فيها أنه يملك السكنى كما حققه الشرنبلالي في رسالة سماها «تحقيق السودد فارجع إليها. أقول: وقد ذكر الخصاف أولاً التسوية بين المسألتين ثم فرق بينهما في باب آخر معللاً بأن سكنى من له الاستغلال کسکنى غيره بخلاف العكس لأنه يوجب فيها حقاً لغيره ومن له الاستغلال إذا سكن لا يوجب حقاً لغيره. قوله : (وفي فتح القدير بقوله وليس الخ هذه العبارة تفيد أنه عند الإطلاق في الوقف يكون للاستغلال وفي النظم الوهباني : ومن وقفت دار عليه فما له سوى الأجر والسكنى بها لا تقرر وتمامه في حاشية الرملي قوله : ( ويدل عليه أي على أن من له الاستغلال ليس له السكني، وبيان الدلالة أن قولهم يصح أن تؤجر الدار للموقوف عليه يدل على أن المراد بالموقوف عليه من له الاستغلال إذ لو كان المراد من له حق السكني لما صح فجواز إجارتها لمن له الاستغلال فقط يدل على أنه ليس له السكنى إذ لا يستأجر لإنسان شيئاً يستحقه. وعبارة البزازية هكذا ولا يملك المصرف السكنى في دار أو حانوت وقفت عليهم بدليل ما ذكره أبو جعفر أن إجارته من المصرف يجوز ومعلوم أن استئجار دار له السكنى لا يجوز فجوازها دل على ما ذكرنا .اهـ. وقوله «له السكنى» «أل» فيه بدل عن الضمير المضاف إليه أي له سكناها. هذا وقد ذكر في البزازية عقب ما قدمناه ما نصه : وفي النوازل وقف عليه غلة دار ليس له السكنى وإن وقف عليه للسكنى لم يكن له الاستغلال اهـ. وهذا هو الموافق لما نقله المؤلف أولاً ووقع في رسالة الشرنبلالي بدون «ليس» فقال عازياً إلى البزازية : وقف عليه غلة دار له السكنى وجعله من جملة ما استدل به على ما قدمناه عنه بناء على أبي أو عجز عمر الحاكم بأجرتها ويصرف نقضه إلى عمارته إن احتاج وإلا حفظه أخذ . وإصلاحها فيما لا بد لها منه فالوقف جائز مع هذا الشرط اهـ. وظاهره أنه يجبر على عمارتها، وقياسه أن الموقوف عليه السكنى كذلك فإن قلت هل يصح بيع العمارة في الأرض الموقوفة؟ قلت قال في القنية من الوقف ويجوز شراء عمارة أرض أو دار للمسجد إذا كانت الرقبة وقفاً وإلا فلا .اهـ. ومن البيوع : ويشترط لجواز بيع العمارة في الحانوت والأشجار في الأرض أن لا يلحقها ضرر بالقلع لأملاك الباعة وفي الوقف لا يشترط، ولو باع بناء واستثنى ما فيه من الخشب أو استثنى ما فيه من اللبن والتراب يجوز إذا اشتراه للنقض اهـ. وفي القنية : دار لسكنى الإمام هدمها وبناها لنفسه وسقفها من الخشب القديم لم يكن له بيع البناء إن بناها كما كانت وفيها أيضاً وقف داراً على إمام مسجد ليسكنه بشرائطه ثم يؤم بنفسه ليس له أن يأخذ أجرتها .اهـ. قوله : ولو أبى أو عجز عمر الحاكم بأجرتها) يعني أجرها الحاكم من الموقوف عليه أو غيره وعمرها بأجرتها ثم يردها بعد التعمير إلى من له السكني لأن في ذلك رعاية للحقين ـ حق الوقف وحق صاحب السكنى ـ لأنه لو لم يعمرها تفوت السكنى أصلاً. أفاد أنه لا يجبر الممتنع على العمارة لما فيه من إتلاف ماله فأشبه امتناع صاحب البذر في المزارعة، ولا يكون امتناعه رضا منه ببطلان حقه لأنه في حيز التردد. وأفاد بقوله «عمر الحاكم بأجرتها أن من له السكنى لا تصح إجارته لأنه غير مالك؛ كذا في الهداية. وأورد عليه أنه إن أراد أنه ليس بمالك للمنفعة وإنما أبيح له الانتفاع كما اختاره في العناية وغاية البيان لزم أن لا يملك الإعارة، والمنقول في الخصاف أنه يملكها فلولا أنه مالك للمنفعة لما ملكها لأنها تمليك المنافع. وإن أراد أنه ليس بمالك للعين والإجارة تتوقف على ملك العين لزم أن لا تصح إجارة المستأجر فيما لا يختلف باختلاف المستعمل وأن لا تصح إعارته وهما صحيحان فالأولى أن يقال كما في فتح القدير لأنه يملك المنافع بلا بدل فلا يملك تمليكها ببدل وهو الإجارة وإلا لملك أكثر مما ملك بخلاف الإعارة، ولا فرق في هذا الحكم أعني عدم الإجارة بين الموقوف عليه السكنى وغيره فلا يملكها السمتحق للغلة أيضاً. ونص الأستروشني أن إجارة الموقوف عليه لا تجوز وإنما يملك الإجارة المتولي أو القاضي. ونقل عن الفقيه أبي جعفر : إن كان الأجر كله للموقوف عليه، فإن كان الوقف لا يسترم ما في النوازل ؛ ذكره البزازي بعد ما قدمه عن أبي جعفر إظهاراً لمخالفته وعلى ما علمته ليس فيه مخالفة له تأمل. قوله : (وظاهره أنه يجبر على عمارتها قال في النهر : الظاهر أنه لا يجبر وسيأتي قريباً ما يؤيده ثم قال بعده قال في الهداية ولا يجبر الممتنع على العمارة لما فيه من إتلاف ماله فأشبه صاحب البذر في المزارعة ولا يكون امتناعه رضا منه ببطلان حقه لأنه في حيز التردد اهـ. وأنت خبير بأن هذا بإطلاقه يشمل ما لو شرط الواقف عليه المرمة لأنها حيث كانت عليه كان في إجباره ما قول المصنف : ولو أبى أو عجز عمر الحاكم) قال في النهر : إتلاف ماله وبهذا اتضح . مر اهـ .
The one who has the right to income does not own the right to dwell. And the one who has the right to dwell does not own the right to income, as is explicitly stated in al-Bazzāziyya and in Fatḥ al-Qadīr with his statement: 'The beneficiaries of the waqf do not have the house for dwelling but for income, just as they do not have the income for dwelling.' End quote. And this is supported by their statement that renting the asset to the beneficiary is valid, and it is known that renting a house from someone who has the right to dwell is not valid, so its validity indicates what we mentioned; thus in al-Bazzāziyya. And I have not seen the ruling when the one with the right to income dwells and does what is not permissible: is rent due upon him and does the administrator take it and then pay it to him? And what appears is that if the waqf needs maintenance, then rent is due upon him, and the administrator takes it to maintain it; otherwise there is no benefit in its obligation since he has no partner in the income. And it is not incumbent upon him only because the administrator rents it out and maintains it with its rent, as if he refused to dwell. But in al-Ẓahīriyya: 'When the waqf is valid and needs maintenance, the maintenance is upon the one who is entitled to the income.' End quote. And it is interpreted to mean that maintenance is from its income, and since its income is his, it becomes that maintenance is upon him. He said in al-Ẓahīriyya: 'If the ones stipulated for the income of the land are a group, and some of them agree that the administrator should repair it from the waqf funds and others refuse, then for whoever wants maintenance, the administrator maintains his share with his share, and for whoever refuses, his share is rented out and its income is spent on maintenance until maintenance is achieved, then it is returned to him.' End quote. And in al-Tātārkhāniyya: 'If the waqf, when stipulating the income for so-and-so as long as he lives, stipulated upon so-and-so its maintenance...' from 'who owns the dwelling' etc. He equated between the two cases, and the second of them is agreed upon, and the first is disputed, and the preponderant in it is that he owns the dwelling, as verified by al-Shirnblāli in a treatise he named 'Taḥqīq al-Sūdad', so refer to it. I say: Al-Khaṣṣāf initially equated between the two cases, then distinguished between them in another chapter, reasoning that dwelling by the one entitled to income is like dwelling by someone else, contrary to the opposite because it creates a right for someone else, and when the one entitled to income dwells, he does not create a right for someone else. His statement: (And in Fatḥ al-Qadīr with his statement 'and not' etc.) This expression indicates that in the absolute waqf, it is for income. And in al-Naẓm al-Wahbāni: 'And for whom a house is endowed, he has nothing but the rent, and dwelling in it is not established.' The rest is in the Ḥāshiya of al-Ramlī. His statement: (And it indicates) i.e., that the one who has the right to income does not have the right to dwell. And the explanation of the indication is that their statement that it is valid to rent out the house to the beneficiary indicates that the intended meaning of 'beneficiary' is the one who has the right to income, because if the intended meaning were the one who has the right to dwell, it would not be valid. So the permissibility of renting it only to the one entitled to income indicates that he does not have the right to dwell, since one does not rent something that he already deserves. And the text of al-Bazzāziyya is as follows: 'And the beneficiary does not own the dwelling in a house or shop endowed upon them, as evidenced by what Abū Ja'far mentioned that renting it from the beneficiary is permissible, and it is known that renting a house in which he has the right to dwell is not permissible, so its permissibility indicates what we mentioned.' End quote. And his statement 'has the right to dwell' — the 'al' is a substitute for the possessive pronoun, i.e., he has its dwelling. This, and it was mentioned in al-Bazzāziyya after what we presented, the text: 'And in al-Nawāzil: If it is endowed upon him for the income of a house, he does not have the right to dwell, and if it is endowed upon him for dwelling, he does not have the right to income.' End quote. And this is consistent with what the author first transmitted, and it occurred in the treatise of al-Shirnblāli without 'not', so he said attributing to al-Bazzāziyya: 'If it is endowed upon him for the income of a house, he has the right to dwell' and he made it part of what he used as evidence for what we presented, based on 'if he refuses or is unable, the ruler rents it and spends its proceeds on its maintenance if needed, otherwise he preserves it.' And its repair in what is indispensable for it, so the waqf is valid with this condition. End quote. And its apparent meaning is that he is forced to maintain it, and by analogy, the beneficiary of dwelling is likewise. If you say: Is it valid to sell the building on the endowed land? I say: It was said in al-Qunyah from the book of waqf: 'It is permissible to purchase a building on land or a house for the mosque if the land is waqf, otherwise no.' End quote. And from the book of sales: 'For the validity of selling the building in the shop and trees on the land, it is required that no harm befall them from uprooting for the properties of the sellers; but in waqf it is not required. And if he sells the building and excludes the wood in it or excludes the bricks and dust, it is permissible if he buys it for demolition.' End quote. And in al-Qunyah: 'If a house for the imam's dwelling is demolished and he builds it for himself and roofs it with old wood, he is not allowed to sell the building if he built it as it was. And also: If he endows a house for the imam of a mosque to dwell in according to its conditions, then he leads prayer himself, he is not allowed to take its rent.' End quote. His statement: (If he refuses or is unable, the ruler rents it and maintains it with its rent) i.e., the ruler rents it to the beneficiary or someone else and maintains it with its rent, then returns it after repair to the one entitled to dwelling, because in this there is regard for both rights — the right of the waqf and the right of the dweller — because if he did not maintain it, the dwelling would be lost entirely. He indicated that the refuser is not forced to maintain, because it would involve destruction of his property, so it resembles the refusal of the owner of seeds in sharecropping, and his refusal is not considered consent to the annulment of his right because he is in a state of hesitation. And he indicated by his statement 'the ruler rents it and maintains it with its rent' that the one entitled to dwelling cannot validly lease it because he is not an owner; thus in al-Hidāya. And it is objected: If he means that he is not an owner of the benefit and only the right to use is permitted to him, as chosen in al-ʿInāya and Ghāyat al-Bayān, then it would follow that he cannot lend it, but it is transmitted in al-Khaṣṣāf that he can lend it, and if he were not an owner of the benefit, he would not be able to lend it because it is a transfer of benefits. And if he means that he is not an owner of the physical asset and leasing depends on ownership of the asset, then it would follow that the lessee's lease is not valid in what does not vary with different users, and that his lending is not valid — both of which are correct. So the better way is to say, as in Fatḥ al-Qadīr, that he owns the benefits without compensation, so he cannot transfer ownership of them for compensation, i.e., lease; otherwise he would own more than what he already owns, unlike lending. And there is no difference in this ruling — i.e., the prohibition of leasing — between the beneficiary of dwelling and others; so the one entitled to income also cannot lease. And the text of al-Ustrūshanī is that the beneficiary's lease is not valid; only the administrator or judge may lease. And it is reported from Faqīh Abū Jaʿfar: 'If the entire rent is for the beneficiary, and if the waqf does not need maintenance, then it is as in al-Nawāzil.' Al-Bazzāzī mentioned it after what he presented from Abū Jaʿfar to show disagreement, but as far as I know, there is no disagreement — ponder. His statement: (And its apparent meaning is that he is forced to maintain it.) He said in al-Nahr: 'The apparent view is that he is not forced, and soon we will mention what supports it.' Then he said after it: 'It was said in al-Hidāya: The refuser is not forced to maintain because it involves destruction of his property, so it resembles the owner of seeds in sharecropping, and his refusal is not considered consent to the annulment of his right because he is in a state of hesitation.' End quote. And you are well aware that this in its absoluteness includes the case where the waqf stipulated the repair upon him, because since it is upon him, forcing him would involve destruction of his property, as the author said: 'If he refuses or is unable, the ruler rents it.' He said in al-Nahr: 'Destruction of his property, and by this it is clear.' End.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharḥ) from al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq expanding the matn of Kanz al-Daqā’iq on the book of Waqf. The passage opens with the central ruling: in a waqf (Islamic endowment), the rights of usufruct (istighlāl, the right to receive income from the property) and dwelling (suknā, the right to inhabit the property) are mutually exclusive. A beneficiary entitled to income cannot dwell in the property, and a beneficiary entitled to dwelling cannot take its income. The commentator then provides the proof from al-Bazzāziyya: it is valid to rent the waqf property to the beneficiary entitled to income. This proves he does not own the right to dwell, because renting a property to someone who already has a right to dwell would be invalid (since you cannot rent what you already own). The same logic applies in reverse. The passage then addresses several subsidiary issues. First, what happens if the income beneficiary dwells unlawfully? The commentator suggests that if the waqf needs maintenance, the administrator can charge him rent equivalent to the market rate and use it for repairs; otherwise, no rent is due as there is no partner in the income. This is linked to the general principle that maintenance costs are borne by the income stream. Second, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Al-Khaṣṣāf initially equated the two rights but later distinguished them, stating that the income beneficiary's dwelling does not create a right for others (since he is still entitled to the income), whereas the dweller's taking income would create a right for someone else (the administrator). The preponderant view (al-rājiḥ) according to al-Shirnblāli is that the income beneficiary also owns the dwelling. However, the majority view in the Ḥanafī school, as presented in Fatḥ al-Qadīr and the Nawāzil, is the mutual exclusivity. The passage also discusses maintenance: if the beneficiary refuses to maintain the property when needed, the administrator rents it out to someone else, uses the rent for maintenance, and then returns it to the beneficiary after repairs. The beneficiary cannot be forced to maintain because that would amount to destruction of his property (itlāf mālihi), analogous to a sharecropper refusing to sow seeds. The discussion extends to leasing: the beneficiary of either right cannot lease the property to others because he does not own the physical asset (ʿayn) and, according to Fatḥ al-Qadīr, he owns the benefit without compensation, so he cannot transfer it for compensation (lease) but can lend it (iʿāra) gratuitously. This is supported by al-Ustrūshanī and the principle that only the administrator or judge may lease waqf property. Finally, the commentator addresses the sale of buildings on waqf land, citing al-Qunyah: it is permissible to purchase such buildings if the land is waqf, but the seller must not harm the structure. The overall authority weight is from the later Ḥanafī fatwā works (al-Bazzāziyya, al-Ẓahīriyya, al-Tātārkhāniyya, Fatḥ al-Qadīr, etc.), which are relied upon in the madhhab.

Key Terms
استغلال (istighlāl): The right to receive the income (ghalla) from a waqf property; usufruct.
سكنى (suknā): The right to dwell in a waqf property.
موقوف عليه (mawqūf ʿalayh): The beneficiary of a waqf; the person entitled to the benefit (income or dwelling) of the endowed property.
إجارة (ijāra): Leasing or renting a property for compensation.
إعارة (iʿāra): Lending a property gratuitously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab as presented in al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq, can a person who is entitled to the income (ghalla) of a waqf house also live in it?
A) Yes, because the right to income entails the right to use the property.
B) No, because the rights are mutually exclusive; the income beneficiary cannot dwell.
C) Yes, but only if he pays rent to the administrator.
D) No, but he can lease it out and collect the rent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf law, the rights to income and dwelling are mutually exclusive: a beneficiary entitled to one cannot exercise the other, as proven by the permissibility of renting the property to the income beneficiary.

Waqf — Endowment
intermediate
8 min
The Qadi's Maintenance of a Waqf Dwelling and the Beneficiary's Inability to Lease
Author rules on qadi's leasing and beneficiary's inability to lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the qadi's authority to lease a waqf dwelling when the beneficiary refuses, and explain why the beneficiary cannot lease it himself using the distinction between i'arah and ijarah.

Core Idea
The qadi acts to preserve the waqf; the beneficiary owns the usufruct without consideration, so he can only lend, not lease.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a practical dilemma: What happens when the beneficiary of a dwelling waqf refuses or is unable to maintain the property? The author explains that the qadi steps in to lease the property and use the rent for maintenance, then returns it to the beneficiary. He also resolves a key legal dispute about the nature of the beneficiary's right—does he own the usufruct or simply have permission to use? The relied-upon Hanafi position is that he owns the usufruct without consideration, which allows him to lend (i'arah) but not to lease (ijarah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem and the qadi's solution
When the beneficiary of a dwelling waqf ('mawqūf 'alayhi al-suknā') refuses or is unable to maintain the property, the text states that the qadi may lease the dwelling to someone else and use the rent to cover necessary repairs. If no maintenance is needed, the qadi preserves the rent for future needs.
This action balances the rights of the waqf (preservation of the corpus) and the beneficiary (right to dwell).
2
Understand why the Beneficiary is not forced to maintain
The author clarifies that the beneficiary is not compelled to spend his own money on repairs. Forcing him would destroy his property (itlaf mal), which is not permissible. This is analogous to the sharecropper who refuses to provide seed—he cannot be forced, nor is his refusal considered a waiver of his right.
The analogy (qiyas) with sharecropping is explicitly drawn in the text.
3
Analyze the qiyas with sharecropping
The qiyas is based on the illa of avoiding destruction of one's property. Just as the sharecropper is not forced to provide seed (asl), the beneficiary is not forced to spend on maintenance (far'). The ruling is transferred because both involve a risk of financial loss to the person.
This qiyas is of type jaliyyah (clear analogy).
4
Examine the dispute about the Beneficiary's ownership of usufruct
The text then addresses a secondary question: Can the beneficiary lease the property himself? Two opinions exist. Some (in al-'Inayah) say he does not own the usufruct at all—he only has permission to use. Others (in Fath al-Qadir) say he owns the usufruct but without consideration (bilā badal). The latter is the relied-upon view.
The proof for ownership is that the beneficiary can give i'arah (lending), which is a transfer of usufruct without compensation.
5
Derive the ruling: Beneficiary cannot lease but can lend
Since the beneficiary owns the usufruct without consideration, he cannot transfer it for a consideration (ijarah). Doing so would give him more than he owns. However, he can lend it (i'arah) because that is a transfer without compensation. Only the mutawalli or qadi has authority to lease the waqf property.
This distinction resolves the objection that if the beneficiary had no ownership, he could not even lend.

كتاب الوقف

أبي أو عجز عمر الحاكم بأجرتها ويصرف نقضه إلى عمارته إن احتاج وإلا حفظه. وإصلاحها فيما لا بد له منها فالوقف جائز مع هذا الشرط اهـ. وظاهره أنه يجبر على عمارتها، وقياسه أن الموقوف عليه السكنى كذلك.
فإن قلت: هل يصح بيع العمارة في الأرض الموقوفة؟ قلت: قال في القنية من الوقف: ويجوز شراء عمارة أرض أو دار للمسجد إذا كانت الرقبة وقفاً وإلا فلا. اهـ. ومن البيوع: ويشترط لجواز بيع العمارة في الحانوت والأشجار في الأرض أن لا يلحقها ضرر بالقلع لأملاك الباعة، وفي الوقف لا يشترط. ولو باع بناء واستثنى ما فيه من الخشب أو استثنى ما فيه من اللبن والتراب يجوز إذا اشتراه للنقض. اهـ. وفي القنية: دار لسكنى الإمام هدمها وبناها لنفسه وسقفها من الخشب القديم لم يكن له بيع البناء إن بناها كما كانت. وفيها أيضاً: وقف داراً على إمام مسجد ليسكنه بشرائطه ثم يؤم بنفسه ليس له أن يأخذ أجرتها. اهـ.
قوله: (ولو أبى أو عجز عمر الحاكم بأجرتها) يعني أجرها الحاكم من الموقوف عليه أو غيره وعمرها بأجرتها ثم يردها بعد التعمير إلى من له السكنى، لأن في ذلك رعاية للحقين — حق الوقف وحق صاحب السكنى — لأنه لو لم يعمرها تفوت السكنى أصلاً. أفاد أنه لا يجبر الممتنع على العمارة لما فيه من إتلاف ماله فأشبه امتناع صاحب البذر في المزارعة، ولا يكون امتناعه رضا منه ببطلان حقه لأنه في حيز التردد. وأفاد بقوله «عمر الحاكم بأجرتها» أن من له السكنى لا تصح إجارته لأنه غير مالك؛ كذا في الهداية. وأورد عليه أنه إن أراد أنه ليس بمالك للمنفعة وإنما أبيح له الانتفاع كما اختاره في العناية وغاية البيان لزم أن لا يملك الإعارة، والمنقول في الخصاف أنه يملكها، فلولا أنه مالك للمنفعة لما ملكها لأنها تمليك المنافع. وإن أراد أنه ليس بمالك للعين والإجارة تتوقف على ملك العين لزم أن لا تصح إجارة المستأجر فيما لا يختلف باختلاف المستعمل وأن لا تصح إعارته، وهما صحيحان، فالأولى أن يقال كما في فتح القدير: لأنه يملك المنافع بلا بدل فلا يملك تمليكها ببدل وهو الإجارة، وإلا لملك أكثر مما ملك بخلاف الإعارة. ولا فرق في هذا الحكم أعني عدم الإجارة بين الموقوف عليه السكنى وغيره، فلا يملكها المستحق للغلة أيضاً. ونص الأستروشني أن إجارة الموقوف عليه لا تجوز، وإنما يملك الإجارة المتولي أو القاضي.
Book of Waqf. If he refuses or is unable, the qadi leases it for its rent and spends it on its maintenance if needed, otherwise preserves it. And repairing it in what is indispensable for it, so the waqf is valid with this condition. And its apparent meaning is that he is forced to maintain it, and by analogy, the beneficiary of the dwelling is likewise. If you ask: Is it valid to sell the building on the waqf land? I say: It is said in al-Qunyah from the chapter of waqf: It is permissible to purchase the building of land or a house for the mosque if the land is waqf, otherwise not. And from the chapter of sales: It is stipulated for the validity of selling the building in a shop and trees on land that no harm comes to them by uprooting for the property of the sellers, but in waqf it is not stipulated. And if he sells a building and exempts the wood in it or exempts the bricks and earth in it, it is permissible if he bought it for demolition. And in al-Qunyah: A house for the residence of the imam, he demolished it and built it for himself and roofed it with old wood, he does not have the right to sell the building if he built it as it was. And also in it: He endowed a house for the imam of a mosque to reside in with its conditions, then he himself leads the prayers, he does not have the right to take its rent. His statement: (And if he refuses or is unable, the qadi leases it for its rent) means the qadi leases it from the beneficiary or someone else and maintains it with its rent, then returns it after maintenance to the one who has the dwelling, because in that is consideration for both rights — the right of the waqf and the right of the owner of the dwelling — because if he did not maintain it, the dwelling would be lost entirely. It indicates that the one who refuses is not forced to maintain it, because of the destruction of his property, so it resembles the refusal of the owner of the seed in sharecropping, and his refusal is not considered consent to the annulment of his right because he is in a state of hesitation. And his statement 'the qadi maintains it with its rent' indicates that the one who has the dwelling cannot validly lease it because he is not an owner; thus in al-Hidayah. And it was objected to him: If he means that he is not an owner of the usufruct but rather the benefit is permitted to him, as chosen in al-'Inayah and Ghayat al-Bayan, then it would follow that he cannot give it as a loan (i'arah), but it is transmitted in al-Khassaf that he can give it as a loan, and if he were not an owner of the usufruct, he would not be able to give it because it is a transfer of usufruct. And if he means that he is not an owner of the corpus and leasing depends on ownership of the corpus, then it would follow that the lessee's leasing is not valid in what does not differ by difference of user, and his lending would not be valid, and both are valid. So it is better to say as in Fath al-Qadir: Because he owns the usufruct without consideration, he cannot transfer it for consideration, which is leasing, otherwise he would own more than what he owns, unlike lending. And there is no difference in this ruling, meaning the invalidity of leasing, between the beneficiary of the dwelling and others, so the one entitled to the produce also cannot lease it. And al-Astarushni explicitly stated that the beneficiary's leasing is not valid, and only the mutawalli or the qadi has the right to lease.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and commentary from Bahr al-Raiq on the chapter of waqf. The author, Ibn al-Nujaym, explains the ruling that when the beneficiary of a dwelling waqf (sukna) refuses or is unable to maintain the property, the qadi may lease it to someone else and use the rent to cover maintenance costs. If no maintenance is needed, the qadi keeps the rent for future needs. The waqf remains valid under this condition. The apparent wording suggests the beneficiary can be forced to maintain, but the author clarifies that he is not forced because forcing him would destroy his property, similar to the sharecropper who refuses to provide seed. The beneficiary's refusal does not forfeit his right.

The author then addresses a secondary issue: Can the beneficiary lease the property himself? The answer is no, because he is not the owner of the usufruct in a way that allows him to transfer it for compensation. This is proven by the fact that he can give it as a loan ('i'arah), which is a transfer of usufruct without compensation. The distinction is that he owns the usufruct without compensation (bilā badal), so he can transfer it without compensation but not with compensation. This reasoning, from Fath al-Qadir, resolves the objection that some scholars thought the beneficiary had no ownership at all. The ruling applies equally to beneficiaries of produce (ghalla). Only the mutawalli or qadi can lease the waqf property.

Key Terms
موقوف عليه السكنى (mawqūf 'alayhi al-suknā): The beneficiary of a dwelling waqf, who has the right to reside in the property.
إجارة (ijārah): Leasing or hiring; a contract transferring usufruct for a known compensation.
إعارة (i'ārah): Lending; a contract transferring usufruct without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the beneficiary of a dwelling waqf not lease the property to someone else for rent?
A) Because he does not own the usufruct at all
B) Because he owns the usufruct without consideration, so he can transfer it only without compensation
C) Because the waqf property cannot be leased under any circumstances
D) Because the qadi always leases it instead

Key Takeaway
The beneficiary of a dwelling waqf cannot lease the property because he owns the usufruct without compensation; the qadi may lease to preserve the waqf.

Compulsion of Maintenance, Leasing Authority, and Istibdal of Waqf
Author discusses compulsion to maintain, leasing authority, and critiques returning to heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compulsion to maintain a waqf, identify the qadi's leasing authority, and explain why istibdal is preferred over returning to heirs.

Core Idea
Istibdal preserves the perpetuity of waqf; returning to heirs contradicts its essence.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses three critical issues in waqf administration: whether a beneficiary can be forced to maintain the property, who has authority to lease it, and what to do when the property becomes completely unusable. The author clarifies the limits of compulsion, the primacy of the mutawalli's authority, and rejects a view that would allow returning the property to the waqif's heirs, affirming istibdal as the correct solution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three issues discussed
The passage covers compulsion to maintain, leasing authority (mutawalli vs qadi), and the extreme case of a ruined waqf.
The author critiques a proposal from Fath al-Qadir.
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling on compulsion to maintain
Despite apparent wording, the correct position is that the beneficiary cannot be forced to maintain because it would destroy his property (itlaf mal). This applies even if the waqif stipulated maintenance.
Analogized to the owner of seed in sharecropping.
3
Determine the leasing authority hierarchy
For houses and shops, leasing is permissible. For lands, if the waqif stipulated payment of taxes, the beneficiary cannot lease. The mutawalli has primary authority; the qadi steps in only if there is no mutawalli or the mutawalli refuses.
Special authority (mutawalli) is stronger than general authority (qadi).
4
Analyze the extreme case and the Fath al-Qadir proposal
Fath al-Qadir suggested giving the beneficiary a choice to maintain or return the property to the waqif's heirs. The author finds this strange because the madhhab clearly permits istibdal.
Returning to heirs contradicts the perpetual nature of waqf.
5
Understand why istibdal is preferred
Istibdal substitutes the ruined waqf with another property of equal or greater value, preserving the waqf's perpetuity. This is the established view in the madhhab for both land and buildings.
The author explicitly critiques the alternative in Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وظاهره أنه يجبر على عمارتها) قال في النهر: الظاهر أنه لا يجبر وسيأتي قريباً ما يؤيده. ثم قال بعده: قال في الهداية: ولا يجبر الممتنع على العمارة لما فيه من إتلاف ماله فأشبه صاحب البذر في المزارعة، ولا يكون امتناعه رضا منه ببطلان حقه لأنه في حيز التردد. اهـ. وأنت خبير بأن هذا بإطلاقه يشمل ما لو شرط الواقف عليه المرمة، لأنها حيث كانت عليه كان في إجباره إتلاف ماله. وبهذا اتضح.

تجوز إجارته وهذا في الدور والحوانيت. وأما الأراضي: فإن كان الواقف شرط تقديم العشر والخراج وسائر المؤن فليس للموقوف عليه أن يؤاجر، وإن لم يشترط ذلك يجب أن يجوز ويكون الخراج والمؤنة عليه، والدعوى من الموقوف عليه غير مسموعة على الصحيح وبه يفتى؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين. فإن قلت: إذا لم يصح إيجاره ما حكم الأجرة إذا آجرها؟ قلت: أن تكون للوقف، ولم أره صريحاً. ولو قالوا عمرها المتولي أو القاضي لكان أولى. فظاهر قولهم إنما يملك الإجارة المتولي أو القاضي أن للقاضي الاستقلال بالإجارة ولو أبى المتولي، إلا أن يكون المراد التوزيع، فالقاضي يؤجرها إن لم يكن لها متول أو كان لها وأبى الأصلح. وأما مع حضور المتولي فليس للقاضي ذلك.

ولم يذكر الشارحون حكم العمارة من المتولي أو القاضي هل هي مملوكة لمن له السكنى أو لا؟ وفي المحيط: فإن أجر القيم وأنفق الأجرة في العمارة فتلك العمارة المحدثة تكون لصاحب السكنى، لأن الأجرة بدل المنفعة، وملك المنفعة كانت مستحقة لصاحب السكنى، فكذا بدل المنفعة تكون له، والقيم إنما أجر لأجله. اهـ. ومقتضاه أنه لو مات تكون ميراثاً كما لو عمرها بنفسه. وفي فتح القدير: ولو لم يرض الموقوف عليه السكنى بالعمارة ولم يجد من يستأجرها لم أر حكم هذه في المنقول من المذهب، والحال فيها يؤدي إلى أن تصير نقضاً على الأرض كوماً تسفوه الرياح، وخطر لي أن يخيره القاضي بين يعمرها ليستوفي منفعتها وبين أن يردها إلى ورثة الواقف. اهـ. وهو عجيب لأنهم صرحوا باستبدال الوقف إذا خرب وصار لا ينتفع به، وهو شامل للأرض والدار. قال في الذخيرة: وفي المنتقى قال هشام: سمعت [؟] يعني [؟] بعد [؟] أن [؟] ومعلوم أن المتولي له ذلك أيضاً، وبه صرح في الحاوي. اهـ.

وسيأتي قوله: (ولو قالوا) قال الرملي: أصحاب المتون. قوله: (إلا) أن يكون المراد التوزيع قال الرملي: وهو الظاهر. قوله: (وأما مع حضور المتولي فليس للقاضي ذلك) قال الرملي: سيأتي قريباً أن له ذلك مع وجود المتولي، فتأمله. وقد قال في الأشباه والنظائر في القاعدة السادسة عشرة: الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة، ذكر فروعاً. وعلى هذا لا يملك القاضي التصرف في الوقف مع وجود ناظر ولو من قبله. اهـ. والإجارة تصرف فيه. والذي يظهر أن المراد التوزيع، يعني إن أبي المتولي أو غاب غيبة منقطعة أو لم يكن لها متول يؤجرها القاضي. وسيأتي أن ولاية القاضي متأخرة عن المشروط له وعن وصيه. تنبه. وسيأتي تمام الكلام على هذه المسألة في الورقة الثانية عشرة.

قوله: (وهو عجيب الخ) قال الرملي: كلام الفتح أعز من أن يجد مستبدلاً أولاً، ويحمل على الثاني إن رأى الاستبدال أو عليهما إن لم يره فلا يجب. تأمل. وقد فرق الشيخ المؤلف في رسالة في الاستبدال بين الأرض فأجازه فيها، وبين الدار فلم يجزه، وأتى بأشياء لا تدل على دعواه. وقوله الآتي لكن ظاهر كلام المشايخ أن محل الاستبدال [؟].
His statement: (And its apparent meaning is that he is forced to maintain it) He said in al-Nahr: The apparent meaning is that he is not forced, and soon what supports it will come. Then he said after it: He said in al-Hidayah: The one who refuses is not forced to maintain because of the destruction of his property, so it resembles the owner of the seed in sharecropping, and his refusal is not considered consent to the annulment of his right because he is in a state of hesitation. And you are aware that this, in its generality, includes the case where the waqif stipulated the maintenance upon him, because where it is upon him, forcing him would be destruction of his property. And with this it became clear.

Its leasing is permissible, and this applies to houses and shops. As for lands: If the waqif stipulated the payment of the tithe, kharaj, and all expenses, then the beneficiary cannot lease it; but if he did not stipulate that, then it must be permissible, and the kharaj and expenses are upon him, and the claim of the beneficiary is not heard according to the correct view, and with it fatwa is given; thus in Jami' al-Fusulayn. If you ask: If his leasing is not valid, what is the ruling on the rent if he leases it? I say: It belongs to the waqf, and I have not seen it explicitly. And if they said: The mutawalli or qadi maintains it, it would be better. So the apparent meaning of their statement 'only the mutawalli or qadi has the right to lease' is that the qadi has independent authority to lease even if the mutawalli refuses, unless the intended meaning is distribution, so the qadi leases it if there is no mutawalli or if there is one but he refuses what is better. But if the mutawalli is present, the qadi does not have that.

And the commentators did not mention the ruling on the maintenance by the mutawalli or qadi: whether it is owned by the one who has the dwelling or not? In al-Muhit: If the custodian (qayyim) leases and spends the rent on maintenance, then that newly created maintenance belongs to the owner of the dwelling, because the rent is the substitute for the usufruct, and the ownership of the usufruct was due to the owner of the dwelling, so likewise the substitute for the usufruct belongs to him, and the custodian only leased for his sake. And its implication is that if he dies, it becomes inheritance as if he had maintained it himself. And in Fath al-Qadir: If the beneficiary of the dwelling is not satisfied with the maintenance and does not find anyone to lease it, I have not seen the ruling on this in the transmitted rulings of the madhhab, and the situation leads to it becoming a pile of rubble that the wind blows away, and it occurred to me that the qadi should give him the choice between maintaining it to take its benefit and returning it to the waqif's heirs. It is strange because they explicitly stated the substitution of the waqf when it becomes ruined and no longer beneficial, and that includes land and houses. He said in al-Dhakhirah: And in al-Muntaqa, Hisham said: I heard [؟] meaning [؟] after [؟] that [؟] and it is known that the mutawalli also has that right, and it is stated explicitly in al-Hawi.

And there will come his statement: (And if they said) said al-Ramli: The authors of the texts. His statement: (Except) that the intended meaning is distribution, said al-Ramli: And it is apparent. His statement: (But if the mutawalli is present, the qadi does not have that) said al-Ramli: It will soon come that he has it with the presence of the mutawalli, so consider it. And it is said in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir in the sixteenth principle: The special authority is stronger than the general authority, he mentioned branches. And on this, the qadi does not have the right to act in the waqf in the presence of a supervisor, even if appointed by him. And leasing is an act of disposal in it. And what appears is that the intended meaning is distribution, meaning if the mutawalli refuses or is absent in a disrupted absenteeism, or there is no mutawalli, the qadi leases it. And it will come that the authority of the qadi is secondary to the person stipulated for and his executor. Pay attention. And the complete discussion of this issue will come in the twelfth folio.

His statement: (And it is strange) al-Ramli said: The speech of Fath is too precious to have a substitute first, and it is interpreted on the second if he sees substitution, or on both if he does not see it, so it is not obligatory. Consider. And the author (Shaykh) distinguished in his treatise on substitution between land, permitting it therein, and a house, not permitting it, and brought things that do not support his claim. And his upcoming statement but the apparent speech of the mashayikh is that the place of substitution [؟].
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary from Bahr al-Raiq on waqf. It addresses three main issues:

1. Compulsion to maintain: Despite the apparent wording that the beneficiary can be forced, the correct Hanafi position is that he cannot be forced because forcing him would destroy his property (itlaf mal). This applies even if the waqif stipulated maintenance on the beneficiary, as compelling him would still cause financial loss.

2. Leasing authority: Leasing is permissible for houses and shops. For agricultural land, if the waqif stipulated that the beneficiary must pay the 'ushr and kharaj, then the beneficiary cannot lease the land; otherwise, he can lease it and must pay those taxes himself. The author then discusses the authority to lease: the mutawalli or qadi can lease. The qadi has independent authority only if there is no mutawalli or the mutawalli refuses to act in the best interest. If the mutawalli is present and willing, the qadi does not have the authority, as the special authority (mutawalli) is stronger than the general authority (qadi).

3. Ownership of improvements and istibdal: If the mutawalli or qadi leases the property and uses the rent for maintenance, the improvements belong to the beneficiary (owner of the dwelling) and become his inheritable property. The author then critiques a view from Fath al-Qadir that suggests, in extreme cases where no one will lease and the property is in ruins, the qadi could give the beneficiary the option to maintain or return the property to the waqif's heirs. The author finds this strange because the madhhab clearly permits istibdal (substitution) of a waqf that has become unusable, which applies to both land and buildings. The preferred view is istibdal, not returning to heirs.

The passage clarifies the limits of qadi's authority and the principle that waqf property should be preserved through istibdal rather than returning to private ownership.

Key Terms
استبدال الوقف (istibdāl al-waqf): Substituting a waqf property with another property of equal or greater value when the original becomes unusable.
متولي الوقف (mutawallī al-waqf): The administrator or supervisor of the waqf appointed by the waqif.
قيم (qayyim): Custodian or manager of the waqf (synonym for mutawalli).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the preferred solution when a waqf property becomes completely unusable and cannot be leased?
A) Return the property to the waqif's heirs
B) Leave the property to decay
C) Perform istibdal (substitution) with another property
D) Appoint a new beneficiary

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a waqf becomes entirely unusable, the correct solution is istibdal (substitution) to preserve its perpetuity, not returning it to the waqif's heirs.

Waqf — Endowment
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Leasing Waqf and Authority of Mutawalli and Qadi
Ruling on leasing waqf and authority of mutawalli and qadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a waqf property may be leased and identify the respective authority of the mutawalli and qadi in leasing and managing waqf.

Core Idea
Leasing waqf is permitted unless the waqif stipulated otherwise; the mutawalli leads, the qadi intervenes only when needed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the permissibility of leasing waqf property and the roles of the mutawalli (manager) and qadi (judge). It distinguishes between houses and shops versus lands, depending on the waqif's stipulations. Understanding these rules ensures that waqf assets are managed lawfully and beneficiaries' rights are protected.

Guided Path
1
Classify the property type and the waqif's stipulation
Identify whether the waqf is a house/shop or land, and whether the waqif stipulated that taxes and expenses be paid from the property itself. This determines the initial permissibility of leasing by the beneficiary.
For houses and shops, leasing is generally permissible; for lands, the waqif's stipulation is critical.
2
Determine the beneficiary's right to lease
If the waqif stipulated payment of tithe, land tax, and expenses, the beneficiary cannot lease. If silent, the beneficiary may lease and must bear these costs. The beneficiary's claim of inability to lease is disregarded.
The rent from an invalid lease still belongs to the waqf, not the beneficiary.
3
Analyze the authority to lease: mutawalli vs. qadi
The mutawalli has primary authority. The qadi may lease only if there is no mutawalli or if the mutawalli refuses a beneficial lease. When a mutawalli is present and willing, the qadi has no power to lease.
The qadi's independence is limited; he cannot override the mutawalli capriciously.
4
Examine ownership of improvements made with rent
If the mutawalli or qadi builds on the property using rent money, the building belongs to the beneficiary of the residence. Upon the beneficiary's death, it is inherited as his property.
The rent is a substitute for usufruct, so its use for improvements accrues to the beneficiary.
5
Contemplate extreme cases: ruin and substitution (istibdal)
If the property becomes ruinous and no tenant is found, the qadi may sell it and buy another property as waqf. Some authorities restrict substitution to land, but many allow it for houses as well. The proceeds from demolishing materials are used for repair or held for future need.
Substitution is a last resort to preserve the waqf's purpose.
